</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Ethanol fuel</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saab_9-3_SportCombi_1.8t_BioPower_Facelift_rear.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Saab_9-3_SportCombi_1.8t_BioPower_Facelift_rear.JPG/300px-Saab_9-3_SportCombi_1.8t_BioPower_Facelift_rear.JPG" width="300" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Saab_9-3_SportCombi_1.8t_BioPower_Facelift_rear.JPG/450px-Saab_9-3_SportCombi_1.8t_BioPower_Facelift_rear.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Saab_9-3_SportCombi_1.8t_BioPower_Facelift_rear.JPG/600px-Saab_9-3_SportCombi_1.8t_BioPower_Facelift_rear.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2340" data-file-height="1254" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saab_9-3_SportCombi_1.8t_BioPower_Facelift_rear.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Saab_9-3" title="Saab 9-3">Saab 9-3</a> SportCombi BioPower was the second <a href="/wiki/E85" title="E85">E85</a> <a href="/wiki/Flexifuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Flexifuel">flexifuel</a> model introduced by <a href="/wiki/Saab_Automobile" title="Saab Automobile">Saab</a> in the Swedish market.</div>
</div>
</div>

<p><b>Ethanol fuel</b> is <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethyl alcohol</a>, the same type of <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> found in <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverages" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcoholic beverages">alcoholic beverages</a>, used as <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a>. It is most often used as a <a href="/wiki/Motor_fuel" title="Motor fuel">motor fuel</a>, mainly as a <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> additive for <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>. The first production car running entirely on ethanol was the <a href="/wiki/Fiat_147" title="Fiat 147">Fiat 147</a>, introduced in 1978 in Brazil by <a href="/wiki/Fiat_Automobiles" title="Fiat Automobiles">Fiat</a>. Nowadays, cars are able to run using 100% ethanol fuel or a <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">mix of ethanol and gasoline</a> (aka <a href="/wiki/Flex-fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Flex-fuel">flex-fuel</a>). It is commonly made from biomass such as corn or sugarcane. World ethanol production for transport fuel tripled between 2000 and 2007 from 17<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> liters (4.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;U.S.&#160;gal; 3.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;imp&#160;gal) to more than 52<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> liters (1.4<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup>&#160;U.S.&#160;gal; 1.1<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup>&#160;imp&#160;gal). From 2007 to 2008, the share of ethanol in global gasoline type fuel use increased from 3.7% to 5.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-UNEP2009_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP2009-1">[1]</a></sup> In 2011 worldwide ethanol fuel production reached 22.36<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> <a href="/wiki/US_gallon" class="mw-redirect" title="US gallon">U.S. gallons</a> (8.46<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">liters</a>; 1.862<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> <a href="/wiki/Imperial_gallon" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial gallon">imperial gallons</a>), with the United States as the top producer with 13.9<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> U.S. gallons (5.3<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> liters; 1.16<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> imperial gallons), accounting for 62.2% of global production, followed by Brazil with 5.6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> U.S. gallons (2.1<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> liters; 4.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> imperial gallons).<sup id="cite_ref-RFAOutlook2012_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAOutlook2012-2">[2]</a></sup> Ethanol fuel has a "<a href="/wiki/Gasoline_gallon_equivalent" title="Gasoline gallon equivalent">gasoline gallon equivalency</a>" (GGE) value of 1.5 US gallons (5.7&#160;L; 1.2&#160;imp&#160;gal), which means 1.5 US gallons of ethanol produces the energy of one US gallon (3.8&#160;L; 0.83&#160;imp&#160;gal) of gasoline.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ethanol fuel is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">Brazil</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_the_United_States" title="Ethanol fuel in the United States">United States</a>, and together both countries were responsible for 87.1% of the world's ethanol fuel production in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-RFAOutlook2012_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAOutlook2012-2">[2]</a></sup> Most cars on the road today in the U.S. can run on <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E10_or_less" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">blends of up to 10% ethanol</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and ethanol represented 10% of the U.S. gasoline fuel supply derived from domestic sources in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-RFAOutlook2012_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAOutlook2012-2">[2]</a></sup> Since 1976 the Brazilian government has made it mandatory to blend ethanol with gasoline, and since 2007 the legal blend is around <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E20.2C_E25" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">25% ethanol and 75% gasoline</a> (E25).<sup id="cite_ref-Portaria2007_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Portaria2007-5">[5]</a></sup> By December 2011 <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicles_in_Brazil" title="Flexible-fuel vehicles in Brazil">Brazil had a fleet</a> of 14.8 million <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flex-fuel automobiles and light trucks</a><sup id="cite_ref-ANFAVEA2010_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANFAVEA2010-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ANFAVEA2011_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANFAVEA2011-7">[7]</a></sup> and 1.5 million flex-fuel <a href="/wiki/Motorcycle" title="Motorcycle">motorcycles</a><sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex09_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex09-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex10_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex10-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex11_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex11-10">[10]</a></sup> that regularly use neat ethanol fuel (known as <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E100" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E100</a>).</p>
<p>Bioethanol is a form of quasi-<a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> that can be produced from agricultural <a href="/wiki/Feedstock" class="mw-redirect" title="Feedstock">feedstocks</a>. It can be made from very common <a href="/wiki/Crops" class="mw-redirect" title="Crops">crops</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>. There has been considerable debate about how useful bioethanol is in replacing gasoline. Concerns about its production and use relate to <a href="/wiki/Food_vs._fuel" title="Food vs. fuel">increased food prices</a> due to the large amount of arable land required for crops,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> as well as the energy and pollution balance of the whole cycle of ethanol production, especially from corn.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Recent developments with <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol_commercialization" title="Cellulosic ethanol commercialization">cellulosic ethanol production and commercialization</a> may allay some of these concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">Cellulosic ethanol</a> offers promise because cellulose fibers, a major and universal component in plant cells walls, can be used to produce ethanol.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-16">[16]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a>, cellulosic ethanol could allow ethanol fuels to play a much bigger role in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethanol-3d-stick-structure.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Ethanol-3d-stick-structure.svg/220px-Ethanol-3d-stick-structure.svg.png" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Ethanol-3d-stick-structure.svg/330px-Ethanol-3d-stick-structure.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Ethanol-3d-stick-structure.svg/440px-Ethanol-3d-stick-structure.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="266" data-file-height="174" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethanol-3d-stick-structure.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Structure of ethanol molecule. All bonds are <a href="/wiki/Single_bond" title="Single bond">single bonds</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">ethanol fermentation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> and other sugars in the corn (or sugarcane or other crops) are converted into ethanol and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>.</p>
<dl>
<dd>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub> → 2 C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH+ 2 CO<sub>2</sub> + heat</dd>
</dl>
<p>Ethanol fermentation is not 100% selective with other side products such as acetic acid, glycols and many other products produced. They are mostly removed during ethanol purification. Fermentation takes place in an aqueous solution. The resulting solution has an ethanol content of around 15%. Ethanol is subsequently isolated and purified by a combination of adsorption and distillation.</p>
<p>During combustion, ethanol reacts with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to produce carbon dioxide, water, and heat:</p>
<dl>
<dd>C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH + 3 O<sub>2</sub> → 2 CO<sub>2</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O + heat</dd>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">Starch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> molecules are strings of glucose molecules. It is also possible to generate ethanol out of cellulosic materials. That, however, requires a pretreatment that splits the cellulose into glycose molecules and other sugars that subsequently can be fermented. The resulting product is called <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic ethanol</a>, indicating its source.</p>
<p>Ethanol may also be produced industrially from <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a> by <a href="/wiki/Hydration_reaction" title="Hydration reaction">hydration</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Double_bond" title="Double bond">double bond</a> in the presence of catalysts and high temperature.</p>
<dl>
<dd>C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O → C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH</dd>
</dl>
<p>By far the largest fraction of the global ethanol production, however, is produced by fermentation.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Energy_crop" title="Energy crop">Energy crop</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saccharum-officinarum-harvest.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Saccharum-officinarum-harvest.JPG/220px-Saccharum-officinarum-harvest.JPG" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Saccharum-officinarum-harvest.JPG/330px-Saccharum-officinarum-harvest.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Saccharum-officinarum-harvest.JPG/440px-Saccharum-officinarum-harvest.JPG 2x" data-file-width="708" data-file-height="416" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saccharum-officinarum-harvest.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sugar cane harvest</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornfield_in_South_Africa2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Cornfield_in_South_Africa2.jpg/220px-Cornfield_in_South_Africa2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Cornfield_in_South_Africa2.jpg/330px-Cornfield_in_South_Africa2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Cornfield_in_South_Africa2.jpg/440px-Cornfield_in_South_Africa2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornfield_in_South_Africa2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cornfield in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Panicum_virgatum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Panicum_virgatum.jpg/170px-Panicum_virgatum.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Panicum_virgatum.jpg/255px-Panicum_virgatum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Panicum_virgatum.jpg/340px-Panicum_virgatum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Panicum_virgatum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Switchgrass</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>About 5% of the ethanol produced in the world in 2003 was actually a petroleum product.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> It is made by the catalytic hydration of ethylene with <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>. It can also be obtained via <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a> or <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbide" title="Calcium carbide">calcium carbide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, oil gas, and other sources. Two million short tons (1,786,000 long tons; 1,814,000&#160;t) of petroleum-derived ethanol are produced annually. The principal suppliers are plants in the United States, Europe, and South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Petroleum derived ethanol (synthetic ethanol) is chemically identical to bio-ethanol and can be differentiated only by radiocarbon dating.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bio-ethanol" class="mw-redirect" title="Bio-ethanol">Bio-ethanol</a> is usually obtained from the conversion of carbon-based <a href="/wiki/Feedstock" class="mw-redirect" title="Feedstock">feedstock</a>. Agricultural feedstocks are considered renewable because they get energy from the sun using photosynthesis, provided that all minerals required for growth (such as nitrogen and phosphorus) are returned to the land. Ethanol can be produced from a variety of feedstocks such as <a href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miscanthus" title="Miscanthus">miscanthus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">sugar beet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, grain, <a href="/wiki/Switchgrass" class="mw-redirect" title="Switchgrass">switchgrass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenaf" title="Kenaf">kenaf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunflower" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunflower">sunflower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">molasses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stover" title="Stover">stover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Straw" title="Straw">straw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, other <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>, as well as many types of cellulose waste and harvesting, whichever has the best <a href="/wiki/Well-to-wheel" class="mw-redirect" title="Well-to-wheel">well-to-wheel</a> assessment.</p>
<p>An alternative process to produce bio-ethanol from algae is being developed by the company <a href="/wiki/Algenol" title="Algenol">Algenol</a>. Rather than grow <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and then harvest and ferment it, the algae grow in sunlight and produce ethanol directly, which is removed without killing the algae. It is claimed the process can produce 6,000 U.S. gallons per acre (5,000 imperial gallons per acre; 56,000 liters per hectare) per year compared with 400 US gallons per acre (330&#160;imp&#160;gal/acre; 3,700&#160;L/ha) for corn production.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Currently, the first generation processes for the production of ethanol from corn use only a small part of the corn plant: the corn kernels are taken from the corn plant and only the starch, which represents about 50% of the dry kernel mass, is transformed into ethanol. Two types of second generation processes are under development. The first type uses <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a> <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> to convert the plant cellulose into ethanol while the second type uses <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> to convert the whole plant to either a liquid <a href="/wiki/Bio-oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Bio-oil">bio-oil</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>. Second generation processes can also be used with plants such as grasses, wood or agricultural waste material such as straw.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The basic steps for large-scale production of ethanol are: microbial (<a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>) <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> of sugars, <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a> (requirements vary, see Ethanol fuel mixtures, below), and <a href="/wiki/Denatured_alcohol" title="Denatured alcohol">denaturing</a> (optional). Prior to fermentation, some crops require <a href="/wiki/Saccharification" class="mw-redirect" title="Saccharification">saccharification</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> of carbohydrates such as cellulose and starch into sugars. Saccharification of cellulose is called <a href="/wiki/Cellulolysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Cellulolysis">cellulolysis</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic ethanol</a>). Enzymes are used to convert starch into sugar.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermentation">Fermentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Fermentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">Ethanol fermentation</a></div>
<p>Ethanol is produced by <a href="/wiki/Microbial_fermentation" class="mw-redirect" title="Microbial fermentation">microbial fermentation</a> of the sugar. Microbial fermentation currently only works directly with <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a>. Two major components of plants, <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> and cellulose, are both made of sugars—and can, in principle, be converted to sugars for fermentation. Currently, only the sugar (e.g., sugar cane) and starch (e.g., corn) portions can be economically converted. There is much activity in the area of cellulosic ethanol, where the cellulose part of a plant is broken down to sugars and subsequently converted to ethanol.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distillation">Distillation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Distillation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethanol_plant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Ethanol_plant.jpg/220px-Ethanol_plant.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Ethanol_plant.jpg/330px-Ethanol_plant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Ethanol_plant.jpg/440px-Ethanol_plant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2485" data-file-height="1728" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethanol_plant.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ethanol plant in West <a href="/wiki/Burlington,_Iowa" title="Burlington, Iowa">Burlington, Iowa</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UsinaSantaElisa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/UsinaSantaElisa.jpg/220px-UsinaSantaElisa.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/UsinaSantaElisa.jpg/330px-UsinaSantaElisa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/UsinaSantaElisa.jpg/440px-UsinaSantaElisa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1271" data-file-height="789" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UsinaSantaElisa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ethanol plant in Sertãozinho, Brazil.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For the ethanol to be usable as a fuel, the majority of the water must be removed. Most of the water is removed by <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a>, but the purity is limited to 95–96% due to the formation of a low-boiling water-ethanol <a href="/wiki/Azeotrope" title="Azeotrope">azeotrope</a> with maximum (95.6% m/m (96.5% v/v) ethanol and 4.4% m/m (3.5% v/v) water). This mixture is called hydrous ethanol and can be used as a fuel alone, but unlike <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">anhydrous</a> ethanol, hydrous ethanol is not miscible in all ratios with gasoline, so the water fraction is typically removed in further treatment to burn in combination with gasoline in gasoline engines.<sup id="cite_ref-DSATC_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DSATC-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dehydration">Dehydration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Dehydration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are basically three dehydration processes to remove the water from an <a href="/wiki/Azeotropic" class="mw-redirect" title="Azeotropic">azeotropic</a> ethanol/water mixture. The first process, used in many early fuel ethanol plants, is called <a href="/wiki/Azeotropic_distillation" title="Azeotropic distillation">azeotropic distillation</a> and consists of adding <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexane" title="Cyclohexane">cyclohexane</a> to the mixture. When these components are added to the mixture, it forms a heterogeneous azeotropic mixture in <a href="/wiki/Vapor%E2%80%93liquid_equilibrium" title="Vapor–liquid equilibrium">vapor–liquid-liquid equilibrium</a>, which when distilled produces anhydrous ethanol in the column bottom, and a vapor mixture of water, ethanol, and cyclohexane/benzene.</p>
<p>When condensed, this becomes a two-phase liquid mixture. The heavier phase, poor in the entrainer (benzene or cyclohexane), is stripped of the entrainer and recycled to the feed—while the lighter phase, with condensate from the stripping, is recycled to the second column. Another early method, called <a href="/wiki/Extractive_distillation" title="Extractive distillation">extractive distillation</a>, consists of adding a ternary component that increases ethanol's relative volatility. When the ternary mixture is distilled, it produces anhydrous ethanol on the top stream of the column.</p>
<p>With increasing attention being paid to saving energy, many methods have been proposed that avoid distillation altogether for dehydration. Of these methods, a third method has emerged and has been adopted by the majority of modern ethanol plants. This new process uses <a href="/wiki/Molecular_sieves" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular sieves">molecular sieves</a> to remove water from fuel ethanol. In this process, ethanol vapor under pressure passes through a bed of molecular sieve beads. The bead's pores are sized to allow absorption of water while excluding ethanol. After a period of time, the bed is regenerated under vacuum or in the flow of inert atmosphere (e.g. N<sub>2</sub>) to remove the absorbed water. Two beds are often used so that one is available to absorb water while the other is being regenerated. This dehydration technology can account for energy saving of 3,000 btus/gallon (840 k<a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">J</a>/L) compared to earlier azeotropic distillation.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-production_water_issues">Post-production water issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-production water issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol is <a href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a>, meaning it absorbs water vapor directly from the atmosphere. Because absorbed water dilutes the fuel value of the ethanol and may cause phase separation of ethanol-gasoline blends (which causes engine stall), containers of ethanol fuels must be kept tightly sealed. This high <a href="/wiki/Miscibility" title="Miscibility">miscibility</a> with water means that ethanol cannot be efficiently shipped through modern <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a>, like liquid hydrocarbons, over long distances.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The fraction of water that an ethanol-gasoline fuel can contain without phase separation increases with the percentage of ethanol.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> This shows, for example, that E30 can have up to about 2% water. If there is more than about 71% ethanol, the remainder can be any proportion of water or gasoline and phase separation does not occur. The fuel mileage declines with increased water content. The increased solubility of water with higher ethanol content permits E30 and hydrated ethanol to be put in the same tank since any combination of them always results in a single phase. Somewhat less water is tolerated at lower temperatures. For E10 it is about 0.5% v/v at 70 F and decreases to about 0.23% v/v at −30 F.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumer_production_systems">Consumer production systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Consumer production systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> production systems have been marketed to home and business users for many years, commercialized ethanol production systems designed for end-consumer use have lagged in the marketplace. In 2008, two different companies announced home-scale ethanol production systems. The AFS125 Advanced Fuel System<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> from Allard Research and Development is capable of producing both ethanol and biodiesel in one machine, while the E-100 MicroFueler<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> from E-Fuel Corporation is dedicated to ethanol only.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Engines">Engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Engines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_economy">Fuel economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Fuel economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol contains approx. 34% less energy per unit volume than gasoline, and therefore in theory, burning pure ethanol in a vehicle reduces miles per US gallon 34%, given the same <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a>, compared to burning pure gasoline. However, since ethanol has a higher octane rating, the engine can be made more efficient by raising its compression ratio. Using a variable geometry or twin scroll <a href="/wiki/Turbocharger" title="Turbocharger">turbocharger</a>, the compression ratio can be optimized for the fuel, making fuel economy almost constant for any blend.<sup id="cite_ref-EEREFAQ_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EEREFAQ-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EIAATTF_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIAATTF-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>For E10 (10% ethanol and 90% gasoline), the effect is small (~3%) when compared to conventional gasoline,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and even smaller (1–2%) when compared to oxygenated and reformulated blends.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> For E85 (85% ethanol), the effect becomes significant. E85 produces lower mileage than gasoline, and requires more frequent refueling. Actual performance may vary depending on the vehicle. Based on EPA tests for all 2006 E85 models, the average fuel economy for E85 vehicles resulted 25.56% lower than unleaded gasoline.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethanol_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethanol-34">[34]</a></sup> The EPA-rated mileage of current United States flex-fuel vehicles<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> should be considered when making price comparisons, but E85 is a high performance fuel, with an octane rating of about 94–96, and should be compared to premium.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_start_during_the_winter">Cold start during the winter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cold start during the winter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brazilian_Honda_Civic_Flex_car_09_2008_logo_%26_secondary_gas_tank.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Brazilian_Honda_Civic_Flex_car_09_2008_logo_%26_secondary_gas_tank.jpg/220px-Brazilian_Honda_Civic_Flex_car_09_2008_logo_%26_secondary_gas_tank.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Brazilian_Honda_Civic_Flex_car_09_2008_logo_%26_secondary_gas_tank.jpg/330px-Brazilian_Honda_Civic_Flex_car_09_2008_logo_%26_secondary_gas_tank.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Brazilian_Honda_Civic_Flex_car_09_2008_logo_%26_secondary_gas_tank.jpg/440px-Brazilian_Honda_Civic_Flex_car_09_2008_logo_%26_secondary_gas_tank.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brazilian_Honda_Civic_Flex_car_09_2008_logo_%26_secondary_gas_tank.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Brazilian 2008 <a href="/wiki/Honda_Civic" title="Honda Civic">Honda Civic</a> flex-fuel has outside direct access to the secondary reservoir gasoline tank in the front right side, the corresponding fuel filler door is shown by the arrow.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>High <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">ethanol blends</a> present a problem to achieve enough <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a> for the fuel to evaporate and spark the ignition during cold weather (since ethanol tends to increase fuel <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy_of_vaporization" title="Enthalpy of vaporization">enthalpy of vaporization</a><sup id="cite_ref-Balabin_2007_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balabin_2007-37">[37]</a></sup>). When vapor pressure is below 45 <a href="/wiki/KPa" class="mw-redirect" title="KPa">kPa</a> starting a cold engine becomes difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> To avoid this problem at temperatures below 11&#160;<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a> (52&#160;<a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">°F</a>), and to reduce ethanol higher emissions during cold weather, both the US and the European markets adopted E85 as the maximum blend to be used in their flexible fuel vehicles, and they are optimized to run at such a blend. At places with harsh cold weather, the ethanol blend in the US has a seasonal reduction to <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E70.2C_E75" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E70</a> for these very cold regions, though it is still sold as E85.<sup id="cite_ref-E70green_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E70green-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-E70_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E70-40">[40]</a></sup> At places where temperatures fall below −12&#160;<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a> (10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">°F</a>) during the winter, it is recommended to install an engine heater system, both for gasoline and E85 vehicles. Sweden has a similar seasonal reduction, but the ethanol content in the blend is reduced to <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E70.2C_E75" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E75</a> during the winter months.<sup id="cite_ref-E70_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E70-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-E75_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E75-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brazilian flex fuel vehicles can operate with ethanol mixtures up to <a href="/wiki/Neat_alcohol_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat alcohol fuel">E100</a>, which is <a href="/wiki/Hydrous" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrous">hydrous</a> ethanol (with up to 4% water), which causes vapor pressure to drop faster as compared to E85 vehicles. As a result, Brazilian flex vehicles are built with a small secondary gasoline reservoir located near the engine. During a cold start pure gasoline is injected to avoid starting problems at low temperatures. This provision is particularly necessary for users of Brazil's southern and central regions, where temperatures normally drop below 15&#160;<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a> (59&#160;<a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">°F</a>) during the winter. An improved flex engine generation was launched in 2009 that eliminates the need for the secondary gas storage tank.<sup id="cite_ref-BioEnergia_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BioEnergia-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gazeta_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gazeta-43">[43]</a></sup> In March 2009 <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_do_Brasil" title="Volkswagen do Brasil">Volkswagen do Brasil</a> launched the <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Polo" title="Volkswagen Polo">Polo E-Flex</a>, the first Brazilian flex fuel model without an auxiliary tank for cold start.<sup id="cite_ref-FlexStart1_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlexStart1-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FlexStart2_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlexStart2-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_mixtures">Fuel mixtures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fuel mixtures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">Common ethanol fuel mixtures</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gas_x_%C3%A1lcool_-_70_percent.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Gas_x_%C3%A1lcool_-_70_percent.svg/170px-Gas_x_%C3%A1lcool_-_70_percent.svg.png" width="170" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Gas_x_%C3%A1lcool_-_70_percent.svg/255px-Gas_x_%C3%A1lcool_-_70_percent.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Gas_x_%C3%A1lcool_-_70_percent.svg/340px-Gas_x_%C3%A1lcool_-_70_percent.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="1050" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gas_x_%C3%A1lcool_-_70_percent.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hydrated ethanol × <a href="/wiki/Gasoline_type_C" class="mw-redirect" title="Gasoline type C">gasoline type C</a> price table for use in Brazil</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EPA_E15_warning_label.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/EPA_E15_warning_label.jpg/220px-EPA_E15_warning_label.jpg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/EPA_E15_warning_label.jpg/330px-EPA_E15_warning_label.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/72/EPA_E15_warning_label.jpg 2x" data-file-width="333" data-file-height="278" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EPA_E15_warning_label.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
EPA's E15 label required to be displayed in all E15 fuel dispensers in the U.S.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In many countries cars are mandated to run on mixtures of ethanol. All Brazilian light-duty vehicles are built to operate for an ethanol blend of up to 25% (<a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E25</a>), and since 1993 a federal law requires mixtures between 22% and 25% ethanol, with 25% required as of mid July 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-USP_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USP-46">[46]</a></sup> In the United States all light-duty vehicles are built to operate normally with an ethanol blend of 10% (<a href="/wiki/E10_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E10 fuel">E10</a>). At the end of 2010 over 90 percent of all gasoline sold in the U.S. was blended with ethanol.<sup id="cite_ref-RFA2011_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFA2011-47">[47]</a></sup> In January 2011 the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Environmental_Protection_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Environmental Protection Agency">U.S. Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) issued a waiver to authorize up to 15% of ethanol blended with gasoline (<a href="/wiki/E15_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E15 fuel">E15</a>) to be sold only for cars and light pickup trucks with a <a href="/wiki/Model_year" title="Model year">model year</a> of 2001 or newer.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT1013_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT1013-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USAToday1013_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAToday1013-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning with the model year 1999, an increasing number of vehicles in the world are manufactured with engines that can run on any fuel from 0% ethanol up to 100% ethanol without modification. Many cars and <a href="/wiki/Light_truck" title="Light truck">light trucks</a> (a class containing <a href="/wiki/Minivan" title="Minivan">minivans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sport_utility_vehicle" title="Sport utility vehicle">SUVs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pickup_truck" title="Pickup truck">pickup trucks</a>) are designed to be flexible-fuel vehicles using ethanol blends up to 85% (<a href="/wiki/E85" title="E85">E85</a>) in North America and Europe, and up to 100% (E100) in Brazil. In older model years, their engine systems contained alcohol sensors in the fuel and/or oxygen sensors in the exhaust that provide input to the engine control computer to adjust the fuel injection to achieve <a href="/wiki/Stochiometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Stochiometric">stochiometric</a> (no residual fuel or free oxygen in the exhaust) air-to-fuel ratio for any fuel mix. In newer models, the alcohol sensors have been removed, with the computer using only oxygen and airflow sensor feedback to estimate alcohol content. The engine control computer can also adjust (advance) the ignition timing to achieve a higher output without pre-ignition when it predicts that higher alcohol percentages are present in the fuel being burned. This method is backed up by advanced knock sensors – used in most high performance gasoline engines regardless of whether they are designed to use ethanol or not – that detect pre-ignition and detonation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_engine_configurations">Other engine configurations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Other engine configurations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl>
<dt>ED95 engines</dt>
</dl>
<p>Since 1989 there have also been ethanol engines based on the diesel principle operating in Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-Scania1_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scania1-50">[50]</a></sup> They are used primarily in city buses, but also in distribution trucks and waste collectors. The engines, made by <a href="/wiki/Scania_AB" title="Scania AB">Scania</a>, have a modified compression ratio, and the fuel (known as ED95) used is a mix of 93.6% ethanol and 3.6% ignition improver, and 2.8% <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(food)" title="Denaturation (food)">denaturants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The ignition improver makes it possible for the fuel to ignite in the diesel combustion cycle. It is then also possible to use the energy efficiency of the diesel principle with ethanol. These engines have been used in the United Kingdom by <a href="/wiki/Reading_Transport#Biofuel_usage" class="mw-redirect" title="Reading Transport">Reading Transport</a> but the use of bioethanol fuel is now being phased out.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Dual-fuel direct-injection</dt>
</dl>
<p>A 2004 <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a> study and an earlier paper published by the Society of Automotive Engineers identified a method to exploit the characteristics of fuel ethanol substantially more efficiently than mixing it with gasoline. The method presents the possibility of leveraging the use of alcohol to achieve definite improvement over the cost-effectiveness of hybrid electric. The improvement consists of using dual-fuel direct-injection of pure alcohol (or the azeotrope or E85) and gasoline, in any ratio up to 100% of either, in a turbocharged, high compression-ratio, small-displacement engine having performance similar to an engine having twice the displacement. Each fuel is carried separately, with a much smaller tank for alcohol. The high-compression (for higher efficiency) engine runs on ordinary gasoline under low-power cruise conditions. Alcohol is directly injected into the cylinders (and the gasoline injection simultaneously reduced) only when necessary to suppress ‘knock’ such as when significantly accelerating. Direct cylinder injection raises the already high octane rating of ethanol up to an effective 130. The calculated over-all reduction of gasoline use and CO<sub>2</sub> emission is 30%. The consumer cost payback time shows a 4:1 improvement over turbo-diesel and a 5:1 improvement over hybrid. The problems of water absorption into pre-mixed gasoline (causing phase separation), supply issues of multiple mix ratios and cold-weather starting are also avoided.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Increased thermal efficiency</dt>
</dl>
<p>In a 2008 study, complex engine controls and increased exhaust gas recirculation allowed a compression ratio of 19.5 with fuels ranging from neat ethanol to E50. Thermal efficiency up to approximately that for a diesel was achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> This would result in the <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> of a neat ethanol vehicle to be about the same as one burning gasoline.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Fuel cells powered by an ethanol reformer</dt>
</dl>
<p>In June 2016, <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a> announced plans to develop <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell_vehicle" title="Fuel cell vehicle">fuel cell vehicles</a> powered by ethanol rather than <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, the fuel of choice by the other car manufacturers that have developed and commercialized fuel cell vehicles, such as the <a href="/wiki/Hyundai_Tucson_FCEV" class="mw-redirect" title="Hyundai Tucson FCEV">Hyundai Tucson FCEV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Mirai" title="Toyota Mirai">Toyota Mirai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Honda_FCX_Clarity" title="Honda FCX Clarity">Honda FCX Clarity</a>. The main advantage of this technical approach is that it would be cheaper and easier to deploy the fueling infrastructure than setting up the one required to deliver hydrogen at high pressures, as each hydrogen fueling station cost <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1 million</span> to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$2 million</span> to build.<sup id="cite_ref-NissanEthanol_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NissanEthanol-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nissan plans to create a technology that uses liquid ethanol fuel as a source to generate hydrogen within the vehicle itself. The technology uses heat to reform ethanol into hydrogen to feed what is known as a solid oxide fuel cell (SOFC). The fuel cell generates electricity to supply power to the electric motor driving the wheels, through a battery that handles peak power demands and stores regenerated energy. The vehicle would include a tank for a blend of water and ethanol, which is fed into an onboard reformer that splits it into pure hydrogen and carbon dioxide. According to Nissan, the liquid fuel could be an ethanol-water blend at a 55:45 ratio. Nissan expects to commercialize its technology by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-NissanEthanol_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NissanEthanol-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Experience_by_country">Experience by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Experience by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_by_country" title="Ethanol fuel by country">Ethanol fuel by country</a></div>
<p>The world's top ethanol fuel producers in 2011 were the United States with 13.9<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> US gallons (5.3<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> litres; 1.16<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> imperial gallons) and Brazil with 5.6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> U.S. gallons (2.1<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> liters; 4.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> imperial gallons), accounting together for 87.1% of world production of 22.36<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> U.S. gallons (8.46<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> liters; 1.862<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> imperial gallons).<sup id="cite_ref-RFAOutlook2012_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAOutlook2012-2">[2]</a></sup> Strong incentives, coupled with other industry development initiatives, are giving rise to fledgling ethanol industries in countries such as Germany, Spain, France, Sweden, China, Thailand, Canada, Colombia, India, Australia, and some Central American countries.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_balance">Energy balance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Energy balance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div style="float: right; margin-left: 10px">
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Energy balance<sup id="cite_ref-bourne_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-59">[59]</a></sup></caption>
<tr>
<th style="background:#f3d161;">Country</th>
<th style="background:#f3d161;">Type</th>
<th style="background:#f3d161;">Energy balance</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>United States</td>
<td>Corn ethanol</td>
<td>1.3</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Germany</td>
<td>Biodiesel</td>
<td>2.5</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Brazil</td>
<td>Sugarcane ethanol</td>
<td>8</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>United States</td>
<td>Cellulosic ethanol<sup>†</sup></td>
<td>2–36<sup>††</sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>† experimental, not in commercial production</p>
<p>†† depending on production method</p>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_energy_balance" title="Ethanol fuel energy balance">Ethanol fuel energy balance</a></div>
<p>All biomass goes through at least some of these steps: it needs to be grown, collected, dried, fermented, distilled, and burned. All of these steps require resources and an infrastructure. The total amount of energy input into the process compared to the energy released by burning the resulting ethanol fuel is known as the <b>energy balance</b> (or "<a href="/wiki/Energy_returned_on_energy_invested" title="Energy returned on energy invested">energy returned on energy invested</a>"). Figures compiled in a 2007 by <i><a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="National Geographic Magazine">National Geographic Magazine</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-bourne_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-59">[59]</a></sup> point to modest results for corn ethanol produced in the US: one unit of fossil-fuel energy is required to create 1.3 energy units from the resulting ethanol. The energy balance for sugarcane ethanol produced in Brazil is more favorable, with one unit of fossil-fuel energy required to create 8 from the ethanol. Energy balance estimates are not easily produced, thus numerous such reports have been generated that are contradictory. For instance, a separate survey reports that production of ethanol from sugarcane, which requires a tropical climate to grow productively, returns from 8 to 9 units of energy for each unit expended, as compared to corn, which only returns about 1.34 units of fuel energy for each unit of energy expended.<sup id="cite_ref-BIOFUELS_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIOFUELS-60">[60]</a></sup> A 2006 University of California Berkeley study, after analyzing six separate studies, concluded that producing ethanol from corn uses much less petroleum than producing gasoline.<sup id="cite_ref-UCB_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCB-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">Carbon dioxide</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>, is emitted during fermentation and combustion. This is canceled out by the greater uptake of carbon dioxide by the plants as they grow to produce the biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> When compared to gasoline, depending on the production method, ethanol releases less greenhouse gases.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_pollution">Air pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Air pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Compared with conventional <a href="/wiki/Unleaded_gasoline" class="mw-redirect" title="Unleaded gasoline">unleaded gasoline</a>, ethanol is a particulate-free burning fuel source that combusts with oxygen to form carbon dioxide, carbon monoxide, water and <a href="/wiki/Aldehydes" class="mw-redirect" title="Aldehydes">aldehydes</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(United_States)" title="Clean Air Act (United States)">Clean Air Act</a> requires the addition of <a href="/wiki/Oxygenate" title="Oxygenate">oxygenates</a> to reduce carbon monoxide emissions in the United States. The additive <a href="/wiki/MTBE" class="mw-redirect" title="MTBE">MTBE</a> is currently being phased out due to ground water contamination, hence ethanol becomes an attractive alternative additive. Current production methods include air pollution from the manufacturer of macronutrient <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a> such as ammonia.</p>
<p>A study by atmospheric scientists at Stanford University found that E85 fuel would increase the risk of air pollution deaths relative to gasoline by 9% in Los Angeles, US: a very large, urban, car-based metropolis that is a worst-case scenario.<sup id="cite_ref-stanfordpollutionstudy_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordpollutionstudy-65">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">Ozone</a> levels are significantly increased, thereby increasing photochemical smog and aggravating medical problems such as asthma.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brazil burns significant amounts of ethanol biofuel. Gas <a href="/wiki/Chromatograph" class="mw-redirect" title="Chromatograph">chromatograph</a> studies were performed of ambient air in São Paulo, Brazil, and compared to Osaka, Japan, which does not burn ethanol fuel. Atmospheric Formaldehyde was 160% higher in Brazil, and Acetaldehyde was 260% higher.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Precise_language" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated.">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_dioxide">Carbon dioxide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Carbon dioxide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BioEthanolFootprint.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/BioEthanolFootprint.jpg/350px-BioEthanolFootprint.jpg" width="350" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/BioEthanolFootprint.jpg/525px-BioEthanolFootprint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/BioEthanolFootprint.jpg/700px-BioEthanolFootprint.jpg 2x" data-file-width="959" data-file-height="587" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BioEthanolFootprint.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
UK government calculation of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_intensity" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon intensity">carbon intensity</a> of corn bioethanol grown in the US and burnt in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-UKRTFO_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKRTFO-69">[69]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg/350px-BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg" width="350" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg/525px-BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg/700px-BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="949" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graph of UK figures for the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_intensity" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon intensity">carbon intensity</a> of bioethanol and <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>. This graph assumes that all bioethanols are burnt in their country of origin and that previously existing cropland is used to grow the feedstock.<sup id="cite_ref-UKRTFO_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKRTFO-69">[69]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_fuel_standard" title="Low-carbon fuel standard">Low-carbon fuel standard</a></div>
<p><span class="nowrap">The calculation</span> of exactly how much carbon dioxide is produced in the manufacture of bioethanol is a complex and inexact process, and is highly dependent on the method by which the ethanol is produced and the assumptions made in the calculation. A calculation should include:</p>
<ul>
<li>The cost of growing the feedstock</li>
<li>The cost of transporting the feedstock to the factory</li>
<li>The cost of processing the feedstock into bioethanol</li>
</ul>
<p>Such a calculation may or may not consider the following effects:</p>
<ul>
<li>The cost of the change in land use of the area where the fuel feedstock is grown.</li>
<li>The cost of transportation of the bioethanol from the factory to its point of use</li>
<li>The efficiency of the bioethanol compared with standard gasoline</li>
<li>The amount of carbon dioxide produced at the tail pipe.</li>
<li>The benefits due to the production of useful bi-products, such as <a href="/wiki/Cattle_feed" class="mw-redirect" title="Cattle feed">cattle feed</a> or electricity.</li>
</ul>
<p>The graph on the right shows figures calculated by the UK government for the purposes of the <a href="/wiki/Renewable_transport_fuel_obligation" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable transport fuel obligation">Renewable transport fuel obligation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UKRTFO_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKRTFO-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The January 2006 Science article from UC Berkeley's ERG, estimated reduction from corn ethanol in GHG to be 13% after reviewing a large number of studies. In a correction to that article released shortly after publication, they reduce the estimated value to 7.4%. A <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="National Geographic Magazine">National Geographic Magazine</a> overview article (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-bourne_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-59">[59]</a></sup> puts the figures at 22% less CO<sub>2</sub> emissions in production and use for corn ethanol compared to gasoline and a 56% reduction for cane ethanol. Carmaker Ford reports a 70% reduction in CO<sub>2</sub> emissions with bioethanol compared to petrol for one of their flexible-fuel vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>An additional complication is that production requires tilling new soil<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-71">[71]</a></sup> which produces a one-off release of GHG that it can take decades or centuries of production reductions in GHG emissions to equalize.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> As an example, converting grass lands to corn production for ethanol takes about a century of annual savings to make up for the GHG released from the initial tilling.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Change_in_land_use">Change in land use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Change in land use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indirect_land_use_change_impacts_of_biofuels" title="Indirect land use change impacts of biofuels">Indirect land use change impacts of biofuels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fuel_vs._food" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuel vs. food">Fuel vs. food</a></div>
<p>Large-scale farming is necessary to produce agricultural alcohol and this requires substantial amounts of cultivated land. University of Minnesota researchers report that if all corn grown in the U.S. were used to make ethanol it would displace 12% of current U.S. gasoline consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-cornundrum_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornundrum-73">[73]</a></sup> There are claims that land for ethanol production is acquired through deforestation, while others have observed that areas currently supporting forests are usually not suitable for growing crops.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In any case, farming may involve a decline in soil fertility due to reduction of organic matter,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> a decrease in water availability and quality, an increase in the use of pesticides and fertilizers, and potential dislocation of local communities.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> New technology enables farmers and processors to increasingly produce the same output using less inputs.<sup id="cite_ref-cornundrum_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornundrum-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cellulosic ethanol production is a new approach that may alleviate land use and related concerns. Cellulosic ethanol can be produced from any plant material, potentially doubling yields, in an effort to minimize conflict between food needs vs. fuel needs. Instead of utilizing only the starch by-products from grinding wheat and other crops, cellulosic ethanol production maximizes the use of all plant materials, including gluten. This approach would have a smaller <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> because the amount of energy-intensive fertilisers and fungicides remain the same for higher output of usable material. The technology for producing cellulosic ethanol is currently in the <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol_commercialization" title="Cellulosic ethanol commercialization">commercialization stage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Using_biomass_for_electricity_instead_of_ethanol">Using biomass for electricity instead of ethanol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Using biomass for electricity instead of ethanol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Converting biomass to electricity for charging electric vehicles may be a more "climate-friendly" transportation option than using biomass to produce ethanol fuel, according to an analysis published in Science in May 2009<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Researchers continue to search for more cost-effective developments in both cellulosic ethanol and advanced vehicle batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_costs_of_ethanol_emissions">Health costs of ethanol emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Health costs of ethanol emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>For each billion ethanol-equivalent gallons of fuel produced and combusted in the US, the combined climate-change and health costs are $469 million for gasoline, $472–952 million for corn ethanol depending on biorefinery heat source (natural gas, corn stover, or coal) and technology, but only $123–208 million for cellulosic ethanol depending on feedstock (prairie biomass, Miscanthus, corn stover, or switchgrass).<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency_of_common_crops">Efficiency of common crops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Efficiency of common crops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As ethanol yields improve or different feedstocks are introduced, ethanol production may become more economically feasible in the US. Currently, research on improving ethanol yields from each unit of corn is underway using biotechnology. Also, as long as oil prices remain high, the economical use of other feedstocks, such as <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, become viable. By-products such as straw or wood chips can be converted to ethanol. Fast growing species like <a href="/wiki/Switchgrass" class="mw-redirect" title="Switchgrass">switchgrass</a> can be grown on land not suitable for other cash crops and yield high levels of ethanol per unit area.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
